"},{"id":36278,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/lakers-youngsters-mixed-results-so-far","date":"2019-01-17","title":"Youngsters struggling to shine in Lakers' new, star-powered spotlight","content":" It was LeBron James who \u201cfelt a pop\u201d on Christmas Day and suffered an agonizing groin injury and hasn\u2019t played since. But really now, who\u2019s dealing with pain \u2014 him or his teammates? The Lakers are 4-7 without James, and lost at home to the likes of the bottom-feeding New York Knicks and Cleveland Cavaliers . They have appeared foggy at times while shooting poorly at others, yet because LeBron does so much for them, maybe this shouldn\u2019t come as a surprise. Or should it? While waiting for LeBron to get the medical green light to suit up \u2014 he\u2019s skipping this current road trip to Oklahoma City and Houston \u2014 there are two very evident Lakers issues that a healthy LeBron covered up for the season\u2019s first two months: 1. They didn\u2019t add all the necessary pieces to this team besides LeBron last offseason. 2. Their young core hasn\u2019t developed as much as projections. The first factor is the least important. When they remodeled the team last summer, the Lakers had one priority besides signing LeBron: maintaining future salary-cap flexibility. That meant one-year contracts for any free agents and no trades for players whose contracts would extend multiple years for big dollars. Refuting either would potentially crimp the Lakers\u2019 chances of attacking marquee free agents in 2019, when Kevin Durant and Kawhi Leonard (among others) hit the market . This philosophy was sound, then and now. They would\u2019ve bent this rule for Paul George last summer, but he stayed in Oklahoma City. Why would the Lakers lock themselves into players who could limit their odds of adding one or maybe two A-list names this July? The downside for this budget-minded approach was the Lakers were forced to load up on marginal veterans riding out their sunset years. JaVale McGee, Rajon Rondo, Lance Stephenson, Tyson Chandler and Michael Beasley came cheap and can easily be discarded next summer if better options come along. This group as a whole, though, has produced fairly well \u2014 or as solid as expected (especially Rondo and McGee). Notice, though, that there isn\u2019t a shooter in the group to help fix the Lakers\u2019 biggest weakness (they rank 28th in 3-point field goal percentage ). Yet, this is the failing of team president Magic Johnson and GM Rob Pelinka \u2026 to an extent anyway. Before laying the criticism too thick on the Lakers\u2019 braintrust, understand that Johnson and Pelinka weren\u2019t working with much last summer on the market. Perhaps the only established free-agent shooter who would possibly meet the Lakers\u2019 guidelines was Marco Belinelli, who signed with the San Antonio Spurs instead . The more alarming problem here in LeBron\u2019s absence is the lack of player development. Except for Kyle Kuzma, what young Laker is good enough to ride shotgun with LeBron? In the 11 games without an injured LeBron, the Lakers\u2019 youngsters have collectively looked uninspired. Good stretches and good moments are quickly followed by lapses and mistakes. Kuzma, Lonzo Ball, Brandon Ingram and Josh Hart are all 23 and under and wi th that comes inconsistency. Yet when you add LeBron and invite championship talk in the very near future, the developmental clock \u2014 fair or not \u2014 runs faster. The Lakers are still waiting for someone to have a breakout season. \u201cI believe in this group,\u201d said Lakers coach Luke Walton, and yet there are concerns as well. Here\u2019s a look at how each youngster is fairing \u2026 * * * Lonzo Ball:  Playing point guard next to LeBron might be the NBA\u2019s toughest position, as you are no longer the best option with the ball. It\u2019s even more of a challenge for Ball because he can\u2019t shoot. He\u2019s not, for example, Mario Chalmers, who could play off the ball and therefore made it work with LeBron on the Miami Heat. Ball is often left standing on the perimeter, where he\u2019s not a threat, while LeBron runs the offense. When he does have the ball, Ball rarely attacks the rim and draws contact, which is probably just as well, because he\u2019s shooting 42 percent from the free throw line, by far the lowest among NBA guards. His court vision remains sharp, he ranks No. 8 among guards in rebounds per game (5.4) , and is on pace to demolish his steals total from last season (88). Brandon Ingram:  Of all the young players, the Lakers have more riding on Ingram if only because he\u2019s a year beyond the others and therefore is extension-eligible this summer. The Lakers need to see growth and know what they have in Ingram to make a smart money decision come July. The returns on Ingram after two-plus months is mixed. He\u2019s wiser with the ball around the basket, but his 3-point shooting (30 percent) has fallen off after a career-best 39 percent last season. Walton singled out Ingram (and Ball) last week about his lack of intensity. Overall, Ingram remains a puzzle. Is he a keeper who can be an important piece for a contender, or someone to be dealt before the deadline? Another scenario has the Lakers extending Ingram this summer and then including him in a package to the New Orleans Pelicans for Anthony Davis should he turn down the a super-max extension this summer. Josh Hart:  At times this season, Hart was the best shooter among the young bunch. He has started 20 games this season, but his stats have cooled after a solid October ( 11.9 ppg, 40.5 3-point percentage ) to 7.8 ppg and 17.5 3-point percentage this month . The Lakers value his toughness, and he\u2019s shooting 34.6 percent this season on 3-pointers. In terms of his ceiling, Hart appears to be a very good role player and an asset off the bench.  Kyle Kuzma:  His 41 points against the Pistons last week was not only the most impactful non-LeBron game by any Laker, but one of the most efficient in the NBA this season. Kuzma chopped up the Pistons in 29 minutes, going 16-for-24 \u2026 and then went 4-for-18 in a loss to the Utah Jazz . He\u2019s young and inconsistent, but at least Kuzma\u2019s lapses aren\u2019t as vast. When LeBron is healthy, Kuzma found a useful role finishing plays and saw shots caused by double-teams on LeBron. He\u2019s averaging 18.7 ppg and has averaged 20.8 ppg since Christmas . * * * LeBron\u2019s longest extended absence due to injury came in 2014-15, when he missed eight games due to back and knee aches. James is 34 now and his latest absence could be a sign of what may be a new normal for him as he ages \u2014 no matter how much injury-prevention training he does. That makes it more urgent for Kuzma, Ball and Ingram to step forward. Even if the Lakers land another star this summer to become a more serious title threat, they\u2019re on the clock with LeBron. And it won\u2019t restart until at least two more games, if not more. * * * Shaun Powell has covered the NBA for more than 25 years. He could call or text his peers in the Eastern Conference with a gentle nudge in Dwyane Wade\u2019s direction for when they flesh out their All-Star ballots later this month in choosing the East\u2019s seven reserve spots. The Miami Heat coach could do that. But he won\u2019t. \u201cI\u2019m not that kind of guy,\u201d Spoelstra said Tuesday morning, before his team faced the Bucks at Fiserv Forum in Milwaukee. \u201cIt would come across so shocking and probably disingenuous. When I receive those emails that are not written by the coach, just cookie-cutter letters, I kind of roll my eyes.\u201d If Spoelstra, who loves Wade, can\u2019t get sentimental over his veteran player\u2019s prospects to play in the 2019 All-Star Game , he\u2019s not about to lobby the other 14 who\u2019ll hold power over Wade\u2019s 13th selection. Especially since Wade isn\u2019t feeling it, either, in what he\u2019s announced as his final season. \u201cIt doesn\u2019t matter to me,\u201d the 12-time All-Star said. \u201cOne last hurrah is not going to make or break what I feel about my career. I\u2019ll be down there anyway.\u201d There still is a chance that Wade could be voted in as a starter. He ranked second among East guards through the second week of fan voting results , behind only Kyrie Irving. Fan balloting, which will account for 50 percent of each player\u2019s total, ends Jan. 21. NBA players (25 percent) and media (25 percent) also will contribute to the selection of starters, to be announced Jan. 24. Then the coaches\u2019 choice of reserves will come Jan. 31. \u201cI think it\u2019s cool from a fan perspective who love this game,\u201d Wade added, \u201cthat they have so many favorite players that they always want to see \u2018em on that stage. [But] when it comes to the All-Star, you want the guys that deserve to be an All Star, the best first half of the season, to be able to go and live out a dream. I had that opportunity multiple times.\u201d The dream Wade is living out now is about the 40 games left on Miami\u2019s schedule, plus any postseason. When this is over, it\u2019s over, Father Prime cleaning out his desk and clocking out for Father Time. This last lap through the league has been a season unlike any other for Wade, and for those inside and outside the Heat organization who admire the 6-foot-4 guard and the career he\u2019s built over the past 16 years. \u201cA lot of guys in this locker room are going to be as sad and emotional as him on the last game,\u201d teammate James Johnson said after Miami\u2019s 124-86 loss to the Bucks. \u201cThe fight\u2019s still there. It sounds crazy, but I\u2019m not the only one who notices it \u2013 his want to still get better. And help everybody else get better at the same time.\u201d The native of Robbins, Ill., south of Chicago \u2013 and arguably the greatest pro athlete in south Florida\u2019s history \u2013 has crafted highlights and memories everywhere he\u2019s gone in this league. But Milwaukee, if not first among equals, ranks near the top for him as far as NBA opponents\u2019 venues and crowds. It\u2019s where he went to college, at the Marquette University campus that\u2019s a Steph Curry 3-pointer to the west. It\u2019s where he played, sort of; the Bucks and the Golden Eagles shared MECCA till 1988, then Bradley Center through last season and now Fiserv. Wade never had competed at the city\u2019s new downtown arena before Tuesday, but as he spoke courtside with reporters in the morning, a banner with his No. 3 Marquette jersey hung in the rafters directly overhead.  \u201cI always talk about coming to Marquette \u2013 many years ago now \u2013 with a hope and a dream,\u201d Wade said. \u201cHoping to become good enough to play at the next level, and I was able to accomplish that. \u201cThere\u2019s a lot of memories coming back. Played in the Bradley Center a lot, a lot more memories [there].\u201d Wade played just two seasons at Marquette, but left his mark. Especially in 2002-03 when he averaged 21.5 points and led the school to its third Final Four, and first since Al McGuire\u2019s 1977 championship \u201clast hurrah.\u201d The program had produced some solid professional players during and immediately after McGuire\u2019s heady 13-year run, including Maurice Lucas, Jim Chones, Dean Meminger and Doc Rivers. But in the 20 years after Rivers was drafted in 1983, only five Marquette players got a sniff in the NBA. Starting with Wade, who was the No. 5 pick in 2003, eight players from the program have been drafted, including Wesley Matthews, Jae Crowder and Jimmy Butler, and 14 have drawn NBA paychecks of some duration. \u201cIt was really cool early in his career when coach [Tom] Crean was still at Marquette,\u201d Spoelstra said. \u201cHe\u2019d come to our shootarounds and our practices, and you could just feel the legacy that Dwyane had created.\u201d In years past, the Heat often would hold practices on the Marquette campus in a nod to Wade\u2019s roots and as a moment for his younger teammates. \u201cFor guys to see that connection,\u201d the Miami coach said. This time, Wade got to town the day after the demolition of Bradley Center went public with an impressive implosion of the building\u2019s roof. Wade watched video of it with Johnson. Imagine your old grade school being razed, or seeing your favorite playground turned into a shopping mall. \u201cIt was a little emotional,\u201d Wade said. \u201cThere was a lot of memories go in that explosion for me, y\u2019know?\u201d Wade and his team won\u2019t like the memory they made Tuesday in the new place. In the final game of his 36th year \u2013 Wade turns 37 Thursday \u2013 he entered to a loud cheer midway through the first quarter and stuck around for 10 minutes, then came back for the final 94 seconds. He played another 11 minutes after halftime, but it was hopeless. \u201cAn onslaught,\u201d Spoelstra called it, as the Heat fell to 21-21. Wade finished with nine points on 4-of-7 shooting, with three rebounds and four assists. (He came up empty in the one stat that still intrigues him: Wade needs 28 blocked shots in the final 40 games to match Michael Jordan\u2019s record for guards of 893.) That\u2019s the side of the ball that speaks to Milwaukee coach Mike Budenholzer. \u201cHe does amazing things on the biggest stages,\u201d Budenholzer said. \u201cBe it a postseason game or a regular season game, when he dials it up defensively. It\u2019s amazing some of the things he can do. Whether it be blocking shots or getting into the passing lanes and getting steals. His athleticism, his anticipation \u2026 I\u2019ve been on the wrong side of those steals too many times.\u201d Wade is averaging 13.8 points, 3.5 rebounds and 3.9 assists in 25.3 minutes, shooting 42.2 percent overall. His per-36 numbers have been solid \u2013 19.8 points, 4.9 rebounds, 5.5 assists \u2013 but his efficiency rating is low for him and his days of dominating games by getting to the line are over. He plays now more in the service of the other Heat players, running dribble handoffs for the big men, spacing the floor as much as he can as an average 3-point shooter. He\u2019s no longer what Johnson called \u201cthe always-aggressive D-Wade.\u201d  \u201cThe thing is playing a different role, playing with different guys,\u201d Wade said. \u201cI\u2019m not necessarily always playing to my strengths. I\u2019m playing to their strengths.\u201d The Heat and the Bucks play twice more this season, including March 22 in what looms as Wade\u2019s final regular-season game in Milwaukee. Given the uncertainty of injuries and playoff seedings, though, the teams might meet several more times on the Bucks court. Or the game Tuesday could have been his last there. He swapped jerseys after the game, as has become his custom, with Bucks veteran George Hill. Wade will be back at Fiserv Sunday, when Marquette \u2013 at current coach Steve Wojciechowski\u2019s urging \u2013 honors their greatest hoops alumnus. After Miami plays at Detroit Friday and at Chicago Saturday the entire team will bus to Milwaukee for the school\u2019s 11 a.m. matinee against Providence. Only after a ceremony saluting Wade will the Heat head to Boston for its game Monday. \u201cI was flattered,\u201d Wade said. \u201cJust to be able to come back here and create another memory, another moment, with a total different group of guys, with a different student body. So I\u2019m humbled and appreciative of the opportunity on Sunday to say \u2018Thank you\u2019 once again for all the support.\u201d Miami hasn\u2019t been happy with its record, weighed down by wasted opportunities against mediocre foes. Wade has had his moments, but mostly he has looked like a soon-to-turn-37 perimeter player. Will this farewell season be memorable? \u201cI dunno,\u201d Spoelstra said, \u201cbut I\u2019ll be telling Dwyane Wade stories for the rest of my coaching career. And how he was willing to sacrifice and do different things for the betterment of the team.\u201d Wade said that, even on such a moribund night as Tuesday, his commitment to see this through hasn\u2019t wavered. For 22 minutes, he was able to lose himself in the game that largely has defined him, certainly brought him most of what\u2019s good in his life. \u201cThere are other guys in there who are going to carry this loss, and coach is going to, more than I will,\u201d Wade said. \u201cI\u2019m on the downside now. There\u2019s only 40 games left in my basketball life. For me, I\u2019m just enjoying the process. \u201cThis is probably our third one like this. It\u2019s going quick. We\u2019ll be at the All-Star break before we know it, and it\u2019s downhill from there.\u201d * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. Golden State\u2019s coaching staff has spent the past couple of weeks more intensely planning for Cousins\u2019 return and how he will be incorporated into different rotations throughout games, while also holding regular discussions with Cousins about the plan. \u201cIt\u2019s a relief. It\u2019s been a long year for me, a lot of ups and downs,\u201d Cousins said after practice last week. \u201cBut the finish line is ahead and I\u2019m extremely excited to be back on the floor. Family.\u201d Information from The Associated Press was used in this report."},{"id":36324,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/sixers-wolves-both-better-butler-trade","date":"2019-01-15","title":"Sixers, Wolves both better since Butler trade","content":" * Tonight on NBA TV:  Wolves vs. Sixers, 7 ET Jimmy Butler will face his former team for the first time on Tuesday, as the Minnesota Timberwolves visit the Philadelphia 76ers. This reunion isn\u2019t about the crowd reaction. The Philly crowd will certainly be happy to welcome back Robert Covington (who is out with a right knee bone bruise) and Dario Saric, two starters from the team that made a surprise run to the Eastern Conference semifinals last season . Butler won\u2019t make his return to the Target Center until late March, and we\u2019ll see then how Minnesota fans feel about him in the aftermath of his departure . Tonight, all eyes will be on Butler and his former teammates, the ones he chose to leave behind when he asked to be traded last summer . It took almost eight weeks of distracting drama in Minnesota \u2014 an off-site meeting with then team president Tom Thibodeau, a no-show in training camp , boorish behavior in a scrimmage , missed games for \u201cgeneral soreness,\u201d and a disappointing, 4-9 start \u2014 before the Wolves finally traded Butler , along with Justin Patton, to Philadelphia in exchange for Covington, Saric, Jerryd Bayless and a second-round pick. Nine weeks later, both teams seem to be in a better place. In regard to how well they\u2019ve played since, the trade has worked for both the Sixers and Wolves. They had a combined record of 12-15 at the time of the deal and are a combined 37-23 since . Both still have room to grow over the final 12 weeks of the season. The Sixers want to reach The Finals, and with Butler able to opt out of his contract this summer, they have urgency \u2014 even though Joel Embiid and Ben Simmons are just 24 and 22 years old, respectively. The Wolves, meanwhile, are still very much in the race for a playoff spot in the deep Western Conference, sitting just one game in the loss column behind the eighth-place Jazz. Here\u2019s how each team has fared since the trade \u2026  The early days of Butler\u2019s time in Philadelphia will be remembered for his late-game heroics. Butler hit two (almost identical) game-winning 3-pointers \u2014 one in Charlotte and another in Brooklyn \u2014 in his first seven games with the Sixers. After losing their first game with their new star ( blowing a 16-point, fourth-quarter lead in Orlando on Nov. 14), the Sixers won 10 of their next 12 . That was against a relatively soft (and home-heavy) stretch of schedule, though. And seven of the 10 wins were within five points in the last five minutes . Butler made an immediate impact by helping his new team win those close games, shooting 5-for-10 with the score within five in the last five over that 10-2 stretch, with the two game-winners being the highlights. Butler suffered a groin injury at the end of that stretch and missed the next two games (both losses at home). The Sixers lost five of their last nine games in December, including their Christmas game in Boston , when some late-game offensive struggles \u2014 the Sixers shot 3-for-17 with the score within five points in the last five minutes of the fourth quarter or overtime \u2014 brought questions of offensive fit to the forefront. Embiid hasn\u2019t been happy about having to spend so much time on the perimeter, with Simmons occupying the paint when he doesn\u2019t have the ball. Butler has reportedly voiced concern about his role in the offense as well. The Sixers rank eighth in offensive efficiency since the trade , but they\u2019ve played a lot more games against bottom-10 defenses (14) than they have against top-10 defenses (eight) over that stretch. And in 383 minutes with all three of Simmons, Butler and Embiid on the floor, the Sixers have scored just 105.8 points per 100 possessions , a mark well below the league average (108.7). They\u2019ve been much better offensively (scoring more than 118 points per 100 possessions) in 562 minutes with just two of the three stars on the floor. There\u2019s still a lot to figure out in Philadelphia, and the upcoming schedule will challenge the Sixers to do just that. The Wolves will be both the best team Philly has faced in the last two weeks and the worst team it will face over the next four. Beginning with their visit to Indiana on Thursday, the Sixers will play 12 straight games (and 16 of their next 18) against teams that currently have winning records . They\u2019re just 7-8 against that group thus far.  While the Sixers improved offensively since the trade, the Wolves have taken a step forward on the other end of the floor. In fact, in each of their first nine games after the trade (including one before Covington and Saric suited up), they allowed less than 109 points per 100 possessions , something they had done just once in their 13 games before the trade . The Wolves won nine of their first 12 games after the trade and ranked third defensively over that stretch (having ranked 29th on defense prior to the deal). But, as was the case with the Sixers\u2019 early post-trade success, the Wolves benefited from a friendly stretch of schedule. Ten of those 12 games were at home and seven were against teams that currently have losing records. The Wolves then went 4-9 in a stretch where they played 10 of 13 games on the road. And at the end of that stretch, they lost Covington to the knee injury that will have him missing a seventh straight game tonight. They returned home to beat the Magic and Los Angeles Lakers, but that wasn\u2019t enough for Thibodeau to keep his job. He was fired on Jan. 6 , with the Wolves in 11th place at 19-21 . New coach Ryan Saunders has led the team to two wins in his first three games. The Wolves are one of just three teams that ranks in the top 10 on both ends of the floor since the trade. But they\u2019re just 17-13 ( fifth-best mark in the Western Conference ) over that stretch, in part because they\u2019re 7-8 in games that were within five points in the last five minutes (perhaps missing Butler\u2019s ability to get late-game buckets). Through the various stages of their season, the Wolves\u2019 offense has improved steadily. In 30 post-trade games, Karl-Anthony Towns has averaged 23.5 points on 51 percent shooting , up from 19.9 on 46 percent prior to the trade . But the Minnesota defense has had its ups and downs. If they get stops, they win. The Wolves are 14-0 when they\u2019ve allowed 104 or fewer points per 100 possessions and 7-22 when they\u2019ve allowed more than 104. The opportunity is there to get back to the playoffs and prove to Butler that they didn\u2019t ****ing need him. But the Wolves have one of the league\u2019s toughest remaining schedules, with 24 of their final 39 games against teams that are currently over .500. "},{"id":36314,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/harden-17-consecutive-30-point-games","date":"2019-01-15","title":"Harden moves past Bryant with 17th straight 30-point game","content":" James Harden\u2019s historic scoring streak is soaring to new heights. Entering the second half of the Rockets\u2019 matchup with the Grizzlies, Harden had scored 36 points , giving him 17 straight games with 30 or more points. That surpassed Kobe Bryant\u2019s streak of 16 consecutive 30-point games, achieved in 2003 . \u201cThe Beard\u201d finished with 57 points in the Rockets\u2019 112-94 victory and was virtually unstoppable. He shot 17-of-33 from the field and 17-of-18 from the free throw line. He was 6-of-15 from long distance. His point total was higher than the combined points \u2014 53 \u2014 scored by the Grizzlies starters. The 30-plus streak is the NBA\u2019s longest since Wilt Chamberlain strung together 20 such performances in 1964. Elgin Baylor scored 30 or more in 18 straight games from December 1961 to October 1962. Remarkably, Chamberlain also had three other streaks, according to Elias Sports Bureau: a jaw-dropping 65 games of 30 or more from November 1961 to February 1962; 31 games of 30 or more from February 1961 to December 1962; and 25 games from November 1960 to December 1960. Over his last 17 games, Harden has recorded 702 points and 157 assists. Per Elias Sports Bureau, he joins Chamberlain and Bryant as the only players in NBA history to score 700 or more points over a 17-game span in a single season. In addition to the streak, Harden\u2019s 36 set a career high for a first half and tied his career high for either half. It tied Klay Thompson for the most points in a half this season. The 57 was the second-highest point total for a game in Rockets\u2019 franchise history. The record is owned by, you guessed it, Harden with 60 points on Jan. 30th of last year versus the Magic. The reigning MVP has scored 40 or more 14 times this season, and he\u2019s topped 50 points three times this season. Since the 2014-15 season, Harden has 12 games of 50 or more points \u2014 the most in the NBA. On Sunday night, Harden finished with 38 points as the Magic rallied for a 116-109 victory over the Rockets. Harden was just 1-for-17 on 3-point attempts in that game. He did get fouled three times shooting from beyond the arc and was 9 for 9 from the free throw line on those occasions (15 of 16 overall), but it was never enough to give the Rockets a comfortable advantage. Harden made his first two 3-pointers on Monday night to quickly outdo his 3-point shooting from Sunday and finished 6-of-15. Boston has lost two consecutive games."},{"id":36358,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/marques-johnson-qa-bucks","date":"2019-01-14","title":"Q&A: Bucks legend Johnson not holding on to past glories","content":" By his appearance, his vitality and his work schedule, you might not guess Marques Johnson is fast approaching his 63rd birthday (Feb. 8). A 6-foot-7 forward from UCLA started winning awards and championships at Crenshaw High School in Los Angeles and continued that with the Bruins (1975 NCAA title). He was the first winner in 1977 of the John Wooden Award and that spring, he was drafted No. 3 overall by the Milwaukee Bucks. That puts the start of his NBA career squarely in the first half of league history. His exploits as a five-time All-Star best found on YouTube, the nature of his game (mid-range, arriving two years before the league adopted the 3-point shot) and his Twitter handle \u2014 @olskool8888 \u2013 all holler \u201cvintage.\u201d So does the fact that he again is candidate for the Naismith Memorial Basketball Hall of Fame, for which he has been eligible for nearly 25 years. Johnson stays up to date on the game for his job as one of the Bucks\u2019 TV color analysts, a role that\u2019s been especially fun so far this season. He splits his time between Milwaukee and Los Angeles, where he has racked up 26 acting credits per IMDB.com, including his famous turn as \u201cRaymond\u201d in \u201cWhite Men Can\u2019t Jump \u201d [1992]. His NBA resume is strong enough for Springfield, as he averaged 20.1 points, 7.0 rebounds and 3.6 assists in 691 games. The Bucks were 347-227 in his seven seasons, and were considered one of the NBA\u2019s best-ever uncrowned teams. Johnson spoke recently with NBA.com\u2019s Steve Aschburner about a variety of topics.  * * * Steve Aschburner:  I recently wrote a piece about players who have made or are making the transition into broadcasting you did, and the help they got from a program known as \u201cSportscaster U.\u201d But you\u2019ve been doing this for more than 25 years, long before that type of program was available. Were you self-taught? Marques Johnson:  I feel like I\u2019ve been training all my life. When I was little, I set up my own little basketball court. I\u2019d cut out little cardboard rims and tape \u2018em against the wall of my room. I\u2019d clip out the stats of every game from the L.A. Times, so I\u2019d have matchups like John Havlicek against Dave Gambee. I\u2019d do my \u201cChick Hearn,\u201d rapid-fire play-by-play. Just to give you an idea of where my imagination took me. When I got to UCLA, I had a professor who started a sportscasting class. He wanted some of the athletes to participate. So myself, Rick Walker \u2014 who was one of the \u2018Hogs\u2019 for the Washington Redskins \u2014 and a couple other guys who made it in the NFL, we all took the class. It really opened up my eyes to the possibilities, and I had such a great time doing it. I had a campus TV show that deal with non-sports issues \u2014 I was the host and other students in the class were the producers. So that kind of helped prepare me. By the time I got to Milwaukee, I did a lot of stuff for the local NBC affiliate. I took a camera guy on the road and put together a piece of us going against the Sixers and the Celtics. I interviewed Red Auerbach and Dr. J. So I was preparing myself for a long time to make the transition.  SA:  I guess I overlooked you being a theater arts major. With that, and you living in southern California, that probably explains you the acting roles you\u2019ve had, right? MJ:  Again, growing up, I played Conrad Birdie in a production of \u201cBye, Bye Birdie.\u201d I did some acting at UCLA and at Crenshaw. I was Li\u2019l Abner in \u201cLi\u2019l Abner.\u201dWe did \u201cDamn Yankees.\u201d We did \u201cThe Pajama Game.\u201d So just being around acting class and musical theatre, drama class, in seventh and eighth grade, I fell in love with that at about the same time. It was really my first love, but broadcasting pays the bills. SA:  So humor me for a minute. How did your involvement in \u201cWhite Men Can\u2019t Jump,\u201d and your role as \u201cRaymond,\u201d come about? You even mention him in your Twitter profile, so I\u2019m guessing you get asked about that a lot. MJ:  Here\u2019s what happened: We were doing a film for a guy who was a student at the American Film Institute, and it was based on this L.A. legend Raymond Lewis. A bunch of ballplayers and a bunch of actors, shooting up in the Hollywood hills on cold nights. https:\/\/www.youtube.com\/watch?v Cylk Cozart, who\u2019s an actor, told me he had a director friend by the name of Ron Shelton who he played basketball with at the Hollywood \u201cY.\u201d That\u2019s where Denzel Washington and these other actors used to play. Shelton had a new movie he was getting ready to cast, and there was a role he thought I\u2019d be good for. So Cylk told Ron Shelton about me, and Shelton brought me in to read with Wesley Snipes. I did my prep work and had a scene where I pull a knife out on Wesley Snipes\u2019 character. Now, my dad was a barber and he had these straight razors. I had seen a movie a long time ago where a guy did this fancy maneuver with his razor, pulling it out to brandish it. So I worked on that for days before I went in to read. When I did the scene, I pulled it out of my pocket and did the thing with my hand, then chased Wesley around the office where the audition was. They loved it. But they told me, \u201cLook, next time don\u2019t bring a real razor in here. You could hurt somebody with that thing.\u201d SA:  How do you think the actors did with the basketball scenes? MJ:  We had a six-week training camp at a park in south central Los Angeles \u2014 Rancho Cienega playground, near Dorsey High School. We went 10 to noon, five days a week. They wanted everybody to be in basketball shape. And it gave Wesley and Woody Harrelson a chance to really work on their games and get some skills of their own. Wesley was a great athlete but not a basketball player. He couldn\u2019t get the hang of the three-man weave they had us doing, and every time you messed it up, you had to do it again. So every time he was at the front, you had other guys suddenly needing to tie their shoes, \u201cGo ahead of me, man,\u201d and \u201cI\u2019ve got to tighten my jock strap.\u201d Wesley was like, \u201cJust wait. Give me a couple of weeks, I\u2019ll get this done.\u201d And he did. Woody on the other hand was a gym rat. Great basketball gym rat type of guy. I remember he called this bogus foul on me and I told him, \u201cC\u2019mon, man, that\u2019s a BS foul.\u201d And he was like, \u201cAre you impugning my integrity?! How dare you!\u201d I was like, \u201cLook, man, first of all, I went to Crenshaw. I don\u2019t know what impugn means. And second, I\u2019m not doin\u2019 that. I just thought that was a bullcrap foul.\u201d We had a great time. SA:  I\u2019m thinking that current NBA players might know you better from that movie than from your playing career. MJ:  Even when I was at the Wooden Awards the year Grant Hill and Big Dog [Glenn] Robinson were nominated [in 1994]. We were at the downtown L.A. Athletic Club, and I was the speaker. I got up to the microphone, maybe a year or two after \u201cWhite Men\u201d came out, and I said, \u201cProbably you young guys have no idea what I did as a basketball player, but I\u2019m gonna give you a frame of reference for who I am.\u201d I asked them, \u201cHow many of you saw \u2018White Men Can\u2019t Jump?\u201d They raised their hands. \u201cHow many remember the guy who robbed the liquor store?\u201d They just went crazy. \u201cThat\u2019s Raymond!\u201d I told Grant Hill\u2019s mother, \u201cSee what it\u2019s come down to? Five-time All-Star in the NBA, and I\u2019m the thug who robbed the liquor store to today\u2019s generation.\u201d SA:  I had moved from Milwaukee to Minneapolis in the late \u201880s and kind of lost track of you \u2014 Minnesota didn\u2019t have the Timberwolves yet. So when I saw you on the screen, my reaction was, \u201cMan, Marques has fallen on hard times.\u201d Now I know that was acting!  MJ:  The irony of that scene was, when you get to the league, one of the first stories you hear is about this player named Reggie Harding. He was a roommate of [former NBA senior VP] Rod Thorn\u2019s at one point, a big kid who went straight from high school [in Detroit] to the pros. Don\u2019t ask me how that happened back in those days. He was just this street-life type dude who went into the neighborhood liquor store with a ski mask on. He\u2019s 6-10, 6-11, so the guy in the store is like, \u201cC\u2019mon Reggie, you can\u2019t be doing this, man.\u201d And he was like, \u201cThis ain\u2019t me. This ain\u2019t me!\u201d I immediately thought of that when I read the script, I knew that\u2019s who they were talking about. [Harding was killed in a street shooting in Detroit in September 1972, four years after his brief NBA\/ABA career ended.] SA:  I hadn\u2019t realized how many acting roles you\u2019ve had. MJ:  Yeah, my last one was on \u201cCastle,\u201d where I played one of a group of old-school superheroes, where the star Nathan [Fillion] solved the case. It was a lot of fun. I got to shoot a live machine gun \u2014 they were like, \u201cDo you want a stand-in to do it?\u201d \u2014 but I said, \u201cNah, I got this.\u201d I try to do whatever\u2019s feasible and makes sense. I don\u2019t pursue it as much as I used to. SA:  Any similarity between acting and playing basketball at the highest level? MJ:  It all goes back to what [UCLA coach John] Wooden and my coach in high school, Willie West, and my dad told me about preparation. I compare it to a duck on the pond that\u2019s floating real smooth, but underneath the water, its feet are moving at 100 mph. The easier it looks, the harder the work is. It\u2019s the same thing with acting and with basketball. I\u2019ve learned that from the actors I\u2019ve worked with, like Nick Nolte in \u201cBlue Chips.\u201d I spent time in his trailer and saw him with these voluminous notes on Indiana basketball and every practice, every player. Notes taped to the walls of his trailer, kind of like the mindset of a coach. SA:  In your primary job now with the Bucks now, you get to see Giannis Antetokounmpo\u2019s continued growth. What do you make of him now compared to four years ago when you started coming back to Milwaukee? MJ:  He was good but he hadn\u2019t reached this level of greatness he\u2019s at now. I reminded him of a story from New Year\u2019s Day my first year there [2015-16]. He had seen some footage of me, and we had a practice that day. He was like, \u201cHey man, you were really good. I watched the film and you got buckets. Wow! My New Year\u2019s resolution is to get buckets the way you got buckets!\u201d I reminded him of that a couple of weeks ago. And he and [Bucks play-by-play broadcaster] Jim Paschke had lunch not long ago and he was asking Jim who my game was like. Was it like Tobias Harris? Jim said, \u201cWell, Tobias is not a five-time All-Star.\u201d Jim asked me what he should tell him and I said, \u201cTell him what Alex English told me a few years back, that Dwyane Wade is probably the guy who game is most like mine, among current players.\u201d Giannis loved that.  SA:  Nice that Milwaukee has such a budding young star again, isn\u2019t it? MJ:  Giannis is just a joy to be around. My favorite Giannis story is from last season. They had just lost a game and he had struggled from the line that night. I\u2019m walking to my car in the parking structure, and it\u2019s probably 2 degrees outside, so I\u2019m hurrying to get there. The crowd of people had just been let out of the game, and I see this tall skinny black dude kind of weaving his way through the crowd, and he\u2019s got on [an undershirt] and shorts. I\u2019m like, \u201cWhat\u2019s this fool doing out here half-dressed?\u201d And it was Giannis, running to the practice facility to get up some free throws. It just blew me away. That\u2019s just the mindset he has. It\u2019s really refreshing to be around. SA:  He and the Bucks have taken a big step this season under Mike Budenholzer and his coaching staff \u2026 MJ:  Yeah, but don\u2019t forget Jason Kidd. I\u2019m not a J-Kidd basher. I thought, for how long he coached, he was progressing and eventually would turn into a pretty good coach. You have to give Jason credit for that year he put Giannis at the point guard position, making him the primary ball handler. Giannis really took off then. Jason would stand on a chair at practice to see what Giannis saw when he was on the court. True story. That way he could see and make the proper decisions. SA:  A lot of media folks consider Antetokounmpo a top MVP candidate, maybe even the favorite halfway through the season. Your thoughts?  MJ:  Yeah. James Harden is coming along strong, and Kawhi [Leonard] in Toronto. Paul George in OKC gets some mentions. It\u2019s interesting because Jason Kidd a couple years ago talked about how the difficulty of pronouncing Giannis\u2019 name might work against him. I remember saying on air to Jim, \u201cThat\u2019s going to help him, because it\u2019s so unusual.\u201d The whole \u201cGreek Freak\u201d thing, I thought it would give him some extra recognition. But it\u2019s all about what you do on the court. Giannis, despite the 3-point shooting [struggle], he still finds a way to make a difference. And the value he brings to other guys, like [Malcolm] Brogdon and [Brook] Lopez and the open shots. The operative word in the league now is \u201cgravity.\u201d You pull the defenders toward you and open things up for your teammates. SA:  Do you see him adding a reliable 3-point shot? Does he need it? MJ:  He needs it. And you\u2019ll see it, I think, in the playoffs. The first time we played Miami down there, they really overexaggerated packing it in the paint, and he had like nine points. He\u2019s going to see more and more of that. If the Bucks aren\u2019t knocking down 3-point shots, they become a little vulnerable. But I\u2019ve noticed, when guys sag off of Giannis, he\u2019s able to pick up this head of steam, which makes it almost impossible to keep him out of the lane. With his slender build and his footwork, with his speed and quickness, he\u2019s able to get them shifting, going one direction and then cross back the other direction. The thing with developing a jump shot, it will just be easier for him to score. That\u2019s one thing I was taught early in my career: if I could hit five mid-range jump shots a game, I would average 20 points easy because of my offensive rebounding and ability in transition. So that was my goal, to knock down 3-5 little jumpers, and then the rest of the game would open up for me. The same thing with Giannis. If he can hit those jumpers, the game will become easier and he won\u2019t have to expend the same type of energy SA:  Defenses are doing everything they can to make him work. MJ:  He\u2019s getting kind of the \u201cJordan Rules\u201d approach defensively , where teams are knocking him down, playing him physical and just trying to wear him down. It\u2019s a good, aggressive strategy \u2014 people can have their qualms about whether he\u2019s getting fouled more than the calls being made \u2014 but if you\u2019re playing against him, that\u2019s how you have to play. You have to make him feel you and make him earn those points. SA:  So Wade is in Milwaukee Tuesday, facing the Bucks for the last time in the city where he went to college. I\u2019m curious about the comparison you made between your games. You had a few inches on him and played forward. MJ:  Aw, but he\u2019s 6-5 or so. I\u2019m listed at 6-7 \u2014 I always wanted to be Dr. J\u2019s size \u2014 but I\u2019m really more 6-6. But just as a player who is fundamentally sound, who can do all types of things, a 5-tool player, and who has enough athleticism to pull off a spectacular play every now and then. He\u2019s an efficient scoring machine who\u2019s not a 3-point shooter, and that was kind of my game, working in that mid-range area and getting to the basket with great body control. I\u2019ve been a big admirer since his Marquette days, and what he did in Miami has been nothing short of spectacular. SA:  I think it\u2019s funny that his jersey number has been hanging in the rafters in Milwaukee for years because of his time at Marquette, but we\u2019re still waiting to see yours. Is that going to change? MJ:  Uh \u2026 not yet. [There has been speculation that Johnson might be honored by the Bucks this season, in conjunction with his enshrinement Jan. 24 in the Wisconsin Sports Hall of Fame.] SA:  What stands out when you think back to your game with the Bucks? MJ:  I think about how, in my second year in the league with Alex [English] gone, when Dave Meyers hurt his back, I had a great year. Averaged 25.6 points and supplanted Dr. J on the first all-NBA team [in 1978-79]. Going into my third year [1979-80], Nellie calls me into his office and asks me what I think I\u2019m going to average. I said, \u201cWell, I worked real hard on my jump shot and on my conditioning. I feel like I could average 30 pretty easily this year and seven or eight rebounds.\u201d He said, \u201cI believe you can too. But for the good of this team and the balance, and having Sidney Moncrief and Brian Winters \u2026 I\u2019m going to bring in a couple other pieces, upgrade the center position [the Bucks acquired veteran Bob Lanier in a midseason deal with Detroit ]. So I\u2019m going to need you to average 20, 21 points a game, seven rebounds, play good defense and buy into this balanced scoring system I\u2019m trying to implement. SA:  What did you make of that request?  MJ:  We went back and forth on that \u2014 it wasn\u2019t easy to accept, because scoring was kind of my thing. But if you look at my numbers that year, I was always around 20, 21 points. It\u2019s a thing where being a team player may cost me a chance to get into the Hall of Fame, because I might have had another 5,000 points in my career and then I\u2019m getting close to 20,000. That\u2019s more kind of Hall of Fame numbers than where I am now. But you look at my scoring average, it drops from 25.6 to 21.7, and it was at the request of Don Nelson, doing what they wanted me to do. SA:  It\u2019s true. Your shooting percentage kept going up, but your attempts dropped. MJ:  I didn\u2019t make a stink about it with Nellie. But what I tried to do was become in the playoffs the scorer I was meant to be. That was always my chance to be \u2014 not a ball hog or steal the glory \u2014 but a little more aggressive shooting the ball. That\u2019s why my playoff numbers are a little ahead of my regular season numbers. SA:  Those \u201880s Bucks are considered some of the best teams to never reach The Finals. It was just too much to be powerful Philadelphia and Boston teams in the same postseasons. MJ:  One of my bigger regrets was not even winning a championship but not getting to The Finals. In hindsight, though \u2014 and this hit me about 20 years ago \u2014 I started thinking the quality of the players we were going against. I was being guarded by Bobby Jones and having to guard Dr. J at the other end. I\u2019m going against Kevin McHale with his size and reach, and then I\u2019m guarding Larry Bird. I think of all the talent that these teams possessed, and they were just better than us. I always thought, though, and still feel if we had been able to get a center like Bob Lanier two or three years earlier, that would have made a huge difference. He was still good when we got him, about 15 points, eight rebounds, but a couple years earlier might have changed things. We just couldn\u2019t beat teams that were better than us.  SA:  You got traded to the Clippers in 1984, got back to the All-Star Game in 1986 \u2014 and then had the opposite of a farewell tour when your career ended abruptly. What happened? MJ:  I hurt my neck Nov. 20, 1986. We played the Dallas Mavericks, and my jump shot was clicking, I had like 10 points in the first quarter. I went up for one rebound and a friend of mine heard them say it on radio, \u201cThat\u2019s the highest Marques Johnson has jumped since he\u2019s been a Clipper.\u201d Everything was great. But I got one rebound and went to push the ball up the floor, and my teammate Benoit Benjamin was standing right there at the free throw line. My forehead barreled into his big chest. The last thing I remember seeing was two big zeroes \u2014 he was \u201c00.\u201d It was too late to change direction, and it snapped my neck back. I lost the feeling in my upper body for a few minutes. I went down in a heap, couldn\u2019t move, scared out of my mind thinking I was paralyzed. They carried me out on a stretcher. The feeling came back but I went to a lot of specialists who wanted to do a spinal fusion. There were questions whether I\u2019d be able to play again if I got the surgery or play again if I didn\u2019t get the surgery. I had a protruding disc and there was talk if I took another hit, it could cause some paralysis. So I sat out two years. SA:  That sounds miserable. MJ:  Things got worse. In the interim, I lost a son [Marques Jr., 15 months old] in a drowning accident in our pool that following year, 1987. You can imagine, my head was all messed up. It was the darkest time in my life. Alcohol, all that \u2026 Every summer I\u2019d work out and think about trying to come back. But I\u2019d always chicken out, \u201cNah, I don\u2019t want to risk this neck thing.\u201d Finally, in 1989, I got into my workout routine again. Started going up UCLA and playing with some of the Lakers, Vlade Divac, Magic [Johnson]. This one day, Magic came up to me and was like, \u201cWhat\u2019s goin\u2019 on? You gonna play or what?\u201d I wasn\u2019t sure I still had the ability but he was like, \u201cYou got it man. Get back out there.\u201d So I called up Nellie, he was at Golden State, and told he I wanted to come up there. He had Chris Mullin, Mitch Richmond, Tim Hardaway, that crew, and have him take a look at me. I did well and he told me he wanted to bring me to training camp. But after I got to Golden State, they had some injuries \u2014 Alton Lister went down, Manute Bol went down \u2014 and we lost a lot of size. Nellie told me they had to get somebody bigger and younger who could play some long minutes. But he had arranged through [agent] Warren LeGarie for me to be able to play in Italy if I wanted to. I was disappointed but I told him, \u201cThat\u2019s cool.\u201d [Johnson played 10 games for the Warriors before being waived on Nov. 29, 1989.] SA:  How did you like playing overseas? MJ:  I played in a small town, Udine, Italy. Was scoring about 25 a game, but I knew \u2014 based on playing maybe twice a week but practicing sometimes twice a day \u2014 that I was done. I just didn\u2019t have the motivation. Bob McAdoo was over there, Micheal Ray Richardson, and they kept telling me, \u201cYou can play till you\u2019re 40 over here.\u201d But after that first season, I was like, nah, I\u2019m good. Gotta get back to the States and get on with my life. But I always appreciated what Nellie did. At that point in life, bouncing back from what I\u2019d been going through, I needed that carrot dangled in front of me, reaching out and helping me out from what I was going through emotionally. [Johnson has seven children now, five boys and two girls, from son Kris, 43, to 9-year-old daughter Shiloh.] SA:  You played for two coaching legends \u2014 Wooden late in his career and Nelson near the start of his. What were they like? MJ:  Let me correct you on that. I actually played for three. Willie West is a Hall of Fame nominee. My high school coach at Crenshaw has about 16 city championships, about 10 state championships. I was fortunate \u2014 his second year was my first year. We went undefeated for two years. By the time I got to UCLA, Coach Wooden had won nine national champipmships. So you knew what you were getting into with that. That was a highlight, being around him every day and learning some of the life lessons that didn\u2019t resonate with me while I was there but kind of kicked in when I became an adult. Ten years, 12 years later, when going through adversity. SA:  And Nellie?  MJ:  One thing I appreciated about him \u2014 and it\u2019s the same thing I see with Budenholzer and his staff \u2014 Nellie had this way of approaching me as if I was an empty vessel, and he was going to pour in this NBA basketball knowledge that he had gained in the Boston Celtics organization. He went in with the attitude that you didn\u2019t know the footwork needed coming off screens or anything. And Nellie would tell you, \u201cI was one of the slowest guys in the league, but I was one of the best guys in transition because I knew when to run and how to get a head start on the break.\u201d He was the perfect coach for me. Nellie would ask me in particular, if I\u2019d go out there and try to split two guys and turn the ball over, during the timeout he\u2019d say, \u201cWhat did you see out there?\u201d Well, I thought I could beat this one guy to the right, split the two and, if I could draw the third defender, I could flip it to Brian Winters for an open 20-foot jumper on the left wing. And Nellie\u2019d be like, \u201cOK, man. That\u2019s nothing I could ever speak about doing. But you\u2019ve got that kind of talent, I can buy that. OK, I like that.\u201d As long as you had a plan that fit into what he was trying to do, he was OK with whatever mistake you would make. SA:  We\u2019ve talked before about him using you as a point forward. He and Del Harris, among others, had used frontcourt players to initiate offense. But you claim to have coined the term. MJ:  It was my terminology. It\u2019s funny, I came across on the Internet, Game 6 against the New Jersey Nets in 1984, that was the series where it was created. You could see plays in that game where I\u2019m looking over to the bench, getting the calls from Nellie, bringing the ball up the floor, setting the offense, drawing Bob Lanier\u2019s man and throwing dimes. There\u2019s even one play where [Paul] Pressey and me are coming up the court, and he won\u2019t give me the ball \u2026 Remember, Paul out of Tulsa was a point guard and he played point guard for us the year or so before I left. He was great with the ball in his hands. I was always a small forward who would catch it on the wing and scored. He was used to initiating. SA:  These days we hear about positionless basketball. How do you think your game would have translated \u2014 I notice your career stats are 14-for-92 from 3-point range. MJ:  I was taking end of quarter, end of half, end of shot clock too. If I took 15 3-point attempts in a year, probably 10 or 11 were desperation-type shots. SA:  So flings. The kind of shots guys often don\u2019t quite release in time today, so their shooting percentages don\u2019t suffer. MJ:  The other part is, I worked on what I needed to work on to become a great player back in those days. That was the mid-range to 20-foot jump shot. I got that down pat. If a coach would have said or I would have felt, \u201cYou need to work on this 3-point shot,\u201d I believe in all honesty I could have shot 36, 37 percent. But there was no reason to focus on shooting long-ass 25-foot jumpers when I could get to 16 feet and knock down 75 percent of those. SA:  The game has changed in its physical demands, too. Taking games off for \u201cload management.\u201d Obviously flying charter vs. commercial. What do you make of those changes? MJ:  It amazes me how much emphasis they place on proper rest. And they have these trackers telling the trainers how many miles they\u2019re running. People don\u2019t realize, we had training camp for 30 days, going six, sometimes seven days a week. And we had two-a-days all camp. They weren\u2019t worried about making allowances for the wear and tear. We\u2019d go two practices hard, every day, for the whole time. SA:  Now the league tries to minimize back-to-back games. But in your rookie season, the Bucks played five sets of back-to-back-to-back games. You had a stretch from Dec. 2 to Dec. 10, 1977, when Milwaukee played seven games in nine nights, beginning and ending with games on three consecutive nights. It started with a game at New York sandwiched between two home games. Then you flew to the West Coast to face Lakers, and wrapped up that trip at Golden State, at Seattle and at Denver. MJ:  We got back on Sunday, we had to face the Sixers on Tuesday and Nellie had us practicing on Monday. I\u2019d been playing hard minutes, probably averaging 43 those last three games. I remember, I called up Nellie\u2019s secretary, Jean Schuler, and told her, \u201cI can\u2019t get out of bed, I\u2019m so sore right now.\u201d And she said, \u201cThat\u2019s OK, Marques. Nellie said you\u2019d probably call, and to take the day off.\u201d The next night against Dr. J, I went out and had 29. He had 29 too. [laughs] SA:  So what\u2019s your take on your Hall of Fame candidacy, knowing you\u2019re up again for the Class of 2019? MJ:  My name just kind of stays in nomination. Y\u2019know, I\u2019ve come across some Web sites about guys who are Hall of Fame-worthy based on \u201cwin shares\u201d and other analytics, and they say I am deserving of that. And I probably could have played another four or five years if not for the neck injury, so the numbers would have looked a lot better. But five All-Star Games, first-team all-NBA, second-team NBA a couple of times. I played for some great teams in Milwaukee. There\u2019s an honor in being nominated with Willie West, my high school coach, and Sidney Moncrief. I\u2019d like to see Sidney and myself both get in, but it\u2019s out of my hands. Just got to see what happens. I\u2019m definitely one of those guys who\u2019s not a shoe-in. But looking at some things the other day, I thought, I might get to the point where I become an \u201cearly African-American pioneer.\u201d [laughs] * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. According to Chris Haynes of Yahoo Sports , the veteran big man is eager to secure a buyout from the Bulls in order to join two-time defending-champion Golden State Warriors. Chicago, however, reportedly has no intentions of letting Lopez walk only to bolster the already formidable Warriors. \u201cI don\u2019t think there\u2019s an NBA player that doesn\u2019t want to be on a winning team, a playoff team,\u201d Lopez told Yahoo Sports on Friday. \u201cI feel like I\u2019m kind of smack dab in the middle of the prime of my career. In the 1981-82 season, they set a league record by averaging 126.5 points per game. Two seasons later, they were scoring in bunches again \u2014 and setting records, too. Two months after logging (and losing) the highest-scoring game in NBA history , the Nuggets made some more history on Jan. 11, 1984. The Nuggets beat the San Antonio Spurs 163-155 in the highest-scoring non-OT game in NBA history. (AP) \u2014 LeBron James\u2019 strained groin is turning out to be perhaps the most significant injury of the famously durable superstar\u2019s 16-year NBA career. James likely will miss at least three more games after being cleared to increase his on-court activity in his comeback from the injury, the Lakers announced Thursday after an evaluation by their medical staff. The Lakers say James\u2019 healing is \u201cprogressing,\u201d and the four-time NBA MVP will participate in more functional basketball activities during the upcoming week. If James sits out as expected, he will miss 11 consecutive games before his next update, and he will have been out of action for over three weeks since getting hurt during the Lakers\u2019 win at Golden State on Christmas. Those are significant numbers: James never missed more than 13 games during any of his first 15 NBA seasons, and he never sat out for more than two full weeks during his decorated tenures with the Cavaliers or the Miami Heat. The Lakers and James, who turned 34 last month, are being prudent with this injury to a star famous for playing through all types of physical ailments, both major and minor. He has never missed a playoff game despite innumerable minor knocks, and he played in all 82 games for the Cavs last season while leading the NBA in minutes played in both the regular season and the postseason.
